#### Review Article

#### Open Access

## Abstract

The hippocampus is critical for memory and spatial navigation. The ability to map novel environments, as well as more abstract conceptual relationships, is fundamental to the cognitive flexibility that humans and other animals require to survive in a dynamic world. In this review, we survey recent advances in our understanding of how this flexibility is implemented anatomically and functionally by hippocampal circuitry, during both active exploration (online) and rest (offline). We discuss the advantages and limitations of spike timing–dependent plasticity and the more recently discovered behavioral timescale synaptic plasticity in supporting distinct learning modes in the hippocampus. Finally, we suggest complementary roles for these plasticity types in explaining many-shot and single-shot learning in the hippocampus and discuss how these rules could work together to support the learning of cognitive maps.

**Keyword(s):** [hippocampus](https://www.annualreviews.org/search?value1=%22hippocampus%22&option1=pub_keyword "hippocampus"), [learning](https://www.annualreviews.org/search?value1=%22learning%22&option1=pub_keyword "learning"), [memory](https://www.annualreviews.org/search?value1=%22memory%22&option1=pub_keyword "memory"), [plasticity](https://www.annualreviews.org/search?value1=%22plasticity%22&option1=pub_keyword "plasticity")

/content/journals/10.1146/annurev-neuro-102423-100258

2026-06-19

## Metrics

ABSTRACT

The hippocampus is critical for memory and spatial navigation. The ability to map novel environments, as well as more abstract conceptual relationships, is fundamental to the cognitive flexibility that humans and other animals require to survive in a dynamic world. In this review, we survey recent advances in our understanding of how this flexibility is implemented anatomically and functionally by hippocampal circuitry, during both active exploration (online) and rest (offline). We discuss the advantages and limitations of spike timing–dependent plasticity and the more recently discovered behavioral timescale synaptic plasticity in supporting distinct learning modes in the hippocampus. Finally, we suggest complementary roles for these plasticity types in explaining many-shot and single-shot learning in the hippocampus and discuss how these rules could work together to support the learning of cognitive maps.

Keywords
- [hippocampus](https://www.annualreviews.org/search?option1=pub_keyword&value1=%22hippocampus),
- [plasticity](https://www.annualreviews.org/search?option1=pub_keyword&value1=%22plasticity),
- [learning](https://www.annualreviews.org/search?option1=pub_keyword&value1=%22learning),
- [memory](https://www.annualreviews.org/search?option1=pub_keyword&value1=%22memory)

1.  INTRODUCTION

Learning is a change in the internal state of a system in response to external stimuli that supports adapted responses to those stimuli in the future. Learning in the brain takes different forms depending on the system and region and may be classified by the trajectory it takes from the naïve to the expert state. Single-shot learning involves the acquisition of a reliable, persistent novel memory or ability after a single event. Many-shot learning, in contrast, involves slow maturation with repeated exposure, where it may not be possible to attribute what is learned to any specific individual experience. Episodic memory, centered in the hippocampus, combines many features of both types into the memory of past “happenings and doings,” perhaps the type of learning most familiar to humans ([Tulving 2002](#right-ref-B168)).

The hippocampus ( [Figure 1](#f1) ) is an ancient, four-layer allocortical region in the medial temporal lobe that is involved in spatial navigation ([O'Keefe & Dostrovsky 1971](#right-ref-B126)) and most forms of declarative memory ([Eichenbaum 2000](#right-ref-B34), [Morris et al. 1982](#right-ref-B112), [Scoville & Milner 1957](#right-ref-B151)). Hippocampal circuit anatomy has been reviewed extensively; we refer the reader in particular to the excellent review by [Andersen et al. (2007)](#right-ref-B3) (see also the [Supplemental Material](https://www.annualreviews.org/content/journals/10.1146/annurev-neuro-102423-100258#supplementary_data) ). Complementary to this classic anatomical view, the hippocampus can also be viewed through a functional lens, abstracted from the specifics of the underlying circuit and model organism ( [Figure 1b](#f1) ), which we focus on here. Considered as a black box, the hippocampus receives a continuous stream of sensory input from the cortex. It segments this stream into meaningful concepts, with single cells \[in different contexts referred to as place cells ([O'Keefe & Dostrovsky 1971](#right-ref-B126)), memory cells ([Benna & Fusi 2021](#right-ref-B9)), schema cells ([Baraduc et al. 2019](#right-ref-B6)), concept cells ([Quiroga et al. 2005](#right-ref-B140)), etc.\] indicating with their firing whether a particular concept is present or absent given the current inputs. Sequences of these concepts are learned incrementally from sequential experience and continue to be sampled during resting behaviors. The hippocampus then returns internally generated sequences, including replay and theta sequences, back to the cortex. Downstream areas such as the prefrontal cortex use these hippocampal outputs to guide behavior ([Pfeiffer & Foster 2013](#right-ref-B135)); how these sequences support these behaviors has been closely examined in computational studies ([Mattar & Daw 2018](#right-ref-B102)). Hippocampal replay sequences have inspired a synthetic analog, also termed replay, which has been highly successful in reinforcement learning ([Andrychowicz et al. 2017](#right-ref-B4), [Eysenbach et al. 2019](#right-ref-B40), [Hassabis et al. 2017](#right-ref-B68), [Lillicrap et al. 2015](#right-ref-B90), [Rolnick et al. 2019](#right-ref-B143)). Importantly, the output sequences are not identical to the input sequences in their content or statistics: Sequences that were not observed or were impossible in the input may be outputted, salient features of the input may be omitted, and the distribution of stimuli in replay may differ from their observed distributions. These processes do not necessarily only happen once in the hippocampus—place cells and sequences have been reported in all hippocampal subregions ([Buzsáki 2015](#right-ref-B18)), and while there are important microarchitectural differences between these regions, similar mechanisms are likely to be conserved. The gross architecture of the hippocampus enables each subregion to build upon the output of the previous, applying region-specific transformations at each step (e.g., pattern separation in the dentate gyrus, pattern completion in CA3), with the composition of all regions at the CA1 output node able to represent more complex concepts than regions preceding it in the trisynaptic circuit, analogous to how compositions of simple cells in the primary visual cortex give rise to high-level feature selectivity in higher visual areas ([Grill-Spector & Malach 2004](#right-ref-B62)).

Anatomy and function interact at all levels in the hippocampus to enable single- and many-shot learning. This review is not intended to be an exhaustive survey of learning phenomena in the hippocampus; rather, we review and synthesize recent advances in our understanding of these phenomena through a functional lens, examine how the anatomy of the hippocampal circuit supports these functions, and discuss how higher memory and cognitive functions may emerge from simple primitives.

2.  SINGLE-SHOT LEARNING OF PLACE FIELDS

The ability to generalize from single experiences is a salient feature of human learning, which has historically been difficult to replicate in artificial learning systems ([Fei-Fei et al. 2006](#right-ref-B43), [Lake et al. 2013](#right-ref-B86), [Vinyals et al. 2016](#right-ref-B179)). The well-known role of the hippocampus in forming memories of single experiences ([Andersen et al. 2007](#right-ref-B3), [Milner et al. 1968](#right-ref-B108), [Penfield & Milner 1958](#right-ref-B133), [Tulving 2002](#right-ref-B168)) makes it a natural site in the brain to look for such single-shot learning. In animals exploring a new environment for the first time, cells in the hippocampus (place cells) that are tuned to allocentric locations in the environment ([O'Keefe & Dostrovsky 1971](#right-ref-B126)) emerge and fire bursts selectively in those locations (their place fields) on all subsequent trials. This acquisition of an allocentric concept occurs with only a single exposure and represents a qualitative step change in a cell's dynamics from diffuse, sparse firing to concentrated, burst firing. Many studies have argued that multiple distinct forms of single-shot learning in the brain require the hippocampus ([FeldmanHall et al. 2021](#right-ref-B44), [Moser & Moser 2003](#right-ref-B113), [Nicola & Clopath 2019](#right-ref-B118), [Turner & Onysk 2022](#right-ref-B170), [Van Kesteren et al. 2018](#right-ref-B175)). The sudden emergence of place fields in the hippocampus in single trials in particular has been proposed as a neural substrate for single-shot learning ([Bittner et al. 2017](#right-ref-B15), [Magee & Grienberger 2020](#right-ref-B98), [Milstein et al. 2021](#right-ref-B109)) ( [Figure 2](#f2) ), with implications extending beyond representations of physical space: Place cell–like representations also map nonspatial dimensions such as frequency ([Aronov et al. 2017](#right-ref-B5)), faces (e.g., the classic Jennifer Aniston cell) ([Quiroga et al. 2005](#right-ref-B140)), individual conspecifics ([Oliva et al. 2020](#right-ref-B130)), and conjunctions of local stimuli and choice ([Zhao et al. 2022](#right-ref-B192)). For this reason, several authors ([Benna & Fusi 2021](#right-ref-B9), [Kolibius et al. 2023](#right-ref-B84)) have argued that tuned hippocampal cells may simply be memory cells—rather than a dedicated system for representing physical space, place cell–like representations may be a byproduct of compressing inputs associated with a memory under a sparsity penalty. Therefore, a single-shot learning rule discovered for space could represent a generic single-shot rule for acquiring new memories.

Figure 2  Dendritic tuning supports single-shot learning via behavioral timescale synaptic plasticity (BTSP). (a) Schematic of dendritic and somatic tuning. (b) Inputs to cell shown in panel a. Heights represent the weight of each input. (c) Output of the cell. (d) Color-coded heatmap of spatial firing rates of a CA1 pyramidal cell showing place field emergence after a dendritic plateau (top); somatic membrane potential (gray); and low-pass filtered membrane potential (blue) for laps preceding (lap 10), during (lap 11), and after plateau (lap 27) (bottom). (e) Heterogeneous spatial tuning properties of small-diameter dendrites of CA1 pyramidal cells. Tuning curve correlations are calculated exclusively between connected, coimaged dendrites (to left of each bar chart). Vertical histograms show distributions of minimum circular distances between somatic and dendritic place field centers. Bar widths represent the relative abundance of bin values. Panel d adapted with permission from [Bittner et al. (2017)](#right-ref-B15), and panel e adapted with permission from [O'Hare et al. (2022)](#right-ref-B125). Panels a–c adapted from images created with BioRender.com.

2.1.  Plasticity Rules for Single-Shot Learning

Single-shot learning involves selecting synaptic weights corresponding to certain input features for potentiation and others for depotentiation based on a single experience. The magnitude of the weight change in this plasticity event must be large enough to change the output at one or more cells in a way that is reproducible on subsequent presentations of the same and similar but not identical inputs. An algorithmic and mechanistic understanding of how reliable single-shot learning can be implemented remains elusive in both machine learning and neuroscience. A fundamental challenge of single-shot learning is sufficiently weighting generalizable features while avoiding overfitting to spurious features.

Certain features of classic Hebbian forms of plasticity such as spike timing–dependent plasticity (STDP) are poorly suited to implementing single-shot learning. STDP occurs very quickly, on the timescale of tens of milliseconds ([Bi & Poo 1998](#right-ref-B13), [Dan & Poo 2006](#right-ref-B27), [FeldmanHall et al. 2021](#right-ref-B44), [Froemke et al. 2005](#right-ref-B50), [Markram et al. 1997](#right-ref-B101)). The synaptic weight changes associated with each STDP event are small; therefore, many trials are typically needed to learn via STDP ([Bittner et al. 2017](#right-ref-B15)). Due to the fast integration timescale of STDP, only few action potentials can be taken into account at any given time. Therefore, each individual STDP weight update is highly sensitive to noise and only encodes a small amount of information into the network.

A system that entrusts a large modification in synaptic weights to a single plasticity event can only be useful if the signal guiding the weight changes is reliable. One way to achieve this is by integrating over a longer timescale, effectively computing a weighted average over many noisy single spikes ( [Figure 2b](#f2) , [c](#f2) ). A recently discovered plasticity rule, dubbed behavioral timescale synaptic plasticity (BTSP; [Figure 2d](#f2) ) ([Bittner et al. 2017](#right-ref-B15)), is a promising candidate mechanism for single-shot learning in this regard ( [Figure 2](#f2) ). At baseline, a hippocampal pyramidal cell receives a variety of tuned inputs at their dendrites ( [Figure 2a](#f2) , [b](#f2) ). According to the BTSP model ([Bittner et al. 2017](#right-ref-B15), [Magee & Grienberger 2020](#right-ref-B98)), a CA1 pyramidal cell receives an instructive signal, from either endogenous input ([Grienberger & Magee 2022](#right-ref-B60)) or optogenetic stimulation ([Rolotti et al. 2022a](#right-ref-B144); see also the [Supplemental Material](https://www.annualreviews.org/content/journals/10.1146/annurev-neuro-102423-100258#supplementary_data) ), which evokes a long-lasting plateau potential in its distal apical dendrites, potentiating the weights arriving before and immediately after the instructive signal ( [Figure 2c](#f2) ). The potentiated weights subsequently drive the cell to fire when those inputs are active again, that is, when the animal reenters its newly acquired firing field. Potentiating a large set of weights simultaneously in this way adds another layer of robustness: Even if any individual potentiated synapse is spuriously associated with the firing field, by the law of large numbers it is statistically very unlikely that there will be enough to flip the output. [Li et al. (2023)](#right-ref-B88) extended this work to CA3 cells as well, and computational studies of BTSP ([Milstein et al. 2021](#right-ref-B109)) have shown that this mechanism implements a learning algorithm driven by large population activity rather than pairwise activity. Consistent with a role in single-shot learning, [Priestley et al. (2022)](#right-ref-B139) found that BTSP was more strongly expressed in novel environments.

How credit is assigned to inputs received just prior to the instructive signal under this type of single-shot learning remains an open question: The BTSP model requires persistent eligibility traces to be maintained in cells up to seconds after the instigating input has turned off. The molecular substrate of these traces remains unclear, although a genetic study by [Xiao et al. (2023)](#right-ref-B189) suggested that αCaMKII may be the critical mediator that bridges this temporal gap. The origin of the dendritic plateau potential, which causally gives rise to BTSP ([Bittner et al. 2017](#right-ref-B15), [Rolotti et al. 2022a](#right-ref-B144)), is also unclear, although [Grienberger & Magee (2022)](#right-ref-B60) suggest that entorhinal cortex may provide the critical instructive signal for inducing single-shot-type plasticity in CA1 representations.

2.2.  Dendritic Substrate of Single-Shot Learning

A large body of literature has proposed that dendritic compartmentalization underpins feature selectivity in cortical neurons, supported by in vitro ([Branco et al. 2010](#right-ref-B17), [Gonzalez et al. 2022](#right-ref-B57), [Losonczy & Magee 2006](#right-ref-B94), [Losonczy et al. 2010](#right-ref-B96)) and computational ([Kim et al. 2023](#right-ref-B82), [Poirazi & Papoutsi 2020](#right-ref-B137), [Poirazi et al. 2003](#right-ref-B136), [Ujfalussy et al. 2018](#right-ref-B172)) studies, although it has only recently become possible to directly interrogate dendritic input to pyramidal cells in vivo. Independent feature selectivity of distinct dendrites is well established in sensory cortex ([Jia et al. 2010](#right-ref-B76)). Many groups (e.g., [Chen et al. 2013](#right-ref-B23), [Smith et al. 2013](#right-ref-B155)) have found that tuned local dendritic spikes are predictive of somatic tuning. Using calcium imaging, [Rolotti et al. (2022b)](#right-ref-B145) showed that soma-dendrite coupling depends on several variables, including brain state; in certain states, dendrites exhibit a high degree of independence from their conjugate soma. [O'Hare et al. (2022)](#right-ref-B125) demonstrated that intracellular calcium release regulates dendritic compartmentalization ( [Figure 2e](#f2) ). These results suggest a high degree of diversity in dendritic activity, which we believe serves the function of providing a high-dimensional basis from which a rich set of somatic representations can be constructed ([Liao et al. 2024](#right-ref-B88a)) ( [Figure 2a](#f2) ). Under this framework, place cells acquire feature selectivity by nonlinearly integrating a basis of spatially tuned inputs at their dendrites, and different place field locations correspond to different sets of weights on these inputs. Advances in imaging resolution have made it possible to directly test the mechanisms of this theory: [Fan et al. (2023)](#right-ref-B41) confirmed that the acquisition of new CA1 place fields required potentiation of CA2/3 input, while [Gonzalez et al. (2023)](#right-ref-B58) empirically measured the recorded plasticity kernel, together providing the first synapse-level in vivo demonstration of a mechanism of single-shot learning in the hippocampus.

3.  INCREMENTAL LEARNING OF SEQUENCES AND REPLAY

The hippocampus is also critical for the learning of ordered sequences ([Foster & Knierim 2012](#right-ref-B47), [Poldrack & Rodriguez 2003](#right-ref-B138)). Neural population recordings, first made possible by multielectrode recording and subsequently by calcium imaging (see the [Supplemental Material](https://www.annualreviews.org/content/journals/10.1146/annurev-neuro-102423-100258#supplementary_data) ) and Neuropixels ([Jun et al. 2017](#right-ref-B77), [Steinmetz et al. 2021](#right-ref-B158)), have shown that place cells in the hippocampus fire in predictable sequences as an animal explores an environment ( [Figure 3](#f3) ). Regardless of whether the environment is a 1D treadmill, 2D open field, 3D maze ([Grieves et al. 2021](#right-ref-B61)), open room \[in the case of bats ([Eliav et al. 2021](#right-ref-B37), [Ulanovsky & Moss 2007](#right-ref-B173)) and birds ([Payne et al. 2020](#right-ref-B131))\], or a more abstract, nonspatial environment ([Constantinescu et al. 2016](#right-ref-B25), [Nieh et al. 2021](#right-ref-B119)), the trajectory that the animal traces out is 1D and can thus be encoded in sequences. The factors that determine the length of these sequences and how the brain chooses to segment this continuous trajectory into sequence snippets remain unknown. The hippocampal dependence of sequence learning extends beyond episodic memory—the hippocampus is also required for trace fear conditioning ([McEchron et al. 1998](#right-ref-B104)) and has been implicated in the learning of motor ([Jacobacci et al. 2020](#right-ref-B72)) and acoustic sequences ([Jablonowski et al. 2018](#right-ref-B71)), even when not tied to an explicit memory task.

Figure 3  Online and offline hippocampal sequences. (a) Place cell activity during traversal (left) and rest (right). (b) Zoom in showing sequence activity during online theta (left) and replayed during offline sharp-wave ripples (SWRs) (right). (c) Pairwise sequential relationships are acquired online via Hebbian plasticity and read out during offline replay. (d) Online place cell sequences recorded with two-photon calcium imaging in the hippocampal area CA1. An example of three run laps on a circular treadmill belt shows (i) that the position of the animal (blue line) could be decoded (pink line) using (ii) the deconvolved calcium activity of the 180 pyramidal cells (PCs) imaged during this session. (iii) The wavelet spectrogram of the CA1 local field potential (LFP) shows increases in theta-band power during locomotion. Example of an online place cell sequence, arranged by the cells’ peak firing location on a treadmill belt (iv). Two example population synchrony events occurring during immobility with significant forward (top) or reverse (bottom) replay are shown, including the raw and SWR-filtered LFP traces (gray and red lines), the raster of the participating PCs sorted by place field position and the Bayesian decoded posterior probability of position (tiled twice to account for reward-spanning trajectories) (v). Panel d adapted from [Grosmark et al. (2021)](#right-ref-B64).

[![
                  Figure 3 
               ](https://www.annualreviews.org/docserver/ahah/fulltext/neuro/47/1/ne470187.f3_thmb.gif)](https://www.annualreviews.org/docserver/fulltext/neuro/47/1/ne470187.f3.gif)

[Figure 3   
  
Click to view](https://www.annualreviews.org/docserver/fulltext/neuro/47/1/ne470187.f3.gif)

[Download as PowerPoint](https://www.annualreviews.org/docserver/fulltext/neuro/47/1/ne470187.f3.ppt?mimeType=application/vnd.ms-powerpoint)

During active exploration, the 4–8-Hz theta oscillation dominates hippocampal local field potential. Place cells preferentially fire at specific phases in this oscillation, with predictable precession of each cell's preferred phase—that is, spikes fired by a particular place cell will occur at progressively earlier phases as the animal traverses the cell's place field ([Foster & Wilson 2007](#right-ref-B49)) (see the left side of [Figure 3a](#f3) , [b](#f3) ). These firings are coordinated across the entire population such that a place cell whose field precedes another in the animal's physical trajectory will also spike first in each theta cycle, even if their firing fields are grossly overlapping ( [Figure 3a](#f3) ). This organization permits distance in the sequence to serve as a proxy for real distance ([Dragoi & Buzsáki 2006](#right-ref-B28)). These online sequences are predictive of destination and have been interpreted as reflecting current goals ([Wikenheiser & Redish 2015](#right-ref-B186)).

Subsequently, during periods of rest, whether before, during, or after a task \[including during sleep ([Bendor & Wilson 2012](#right-ref-B8), [Ji & Wilson 2007](#right-ref-B75))\], hippocampal networks recapitulate sequences of activity from awake experience in temporally compressed form, a phenomenon known as replay (see the right side of [Figure 3a](#f3) – [d](#f3) ), coinciding with sharp-wave ripples (SWRs), highly synchronous high-frequency bursts of activity associated with memory consolidation ([Buzsáki 2015](#right-ref-B18)). While the majority of such work has been in rodents, recent work has shown analogous findings in other species, including humans ([Reddy et al. 2021](#right-ref-B141), A.A. [Liu et al. 2022](#right-ref-B92)). Although the precise mechanism underlying replay generation is not known, many candidate models have been proposed ([Buzsáki 2015](#right-ref-B18); [Ecker et al. 2022](#right-ref-B32); [Jahnke et al. 2015](#right-ref-B74); [Liao et al. 2022](#right-ref-B89); [Malerba & Bazhenov 2019](#right-ref-B99); [Milstein et al. 2023](#right-ref-B110); [Nicola & Clopath 2017](#right-ref-B117), [2019](#right-ref-B118); [Taxidis et al. 2012](#right-ref-B164)). Replay is critical for memory consolidation ([Buzsáki 2015](#right-ref-B18), [Fernandez-Ruiz et al. 2019](#right-ref-B45), [Girardeau et al. 2009](#right-ref-B56), [Jadhav et al. 2012](#right-ref-B73)) and has been proposed as a neural signature of planning, allowing animals to leverage memories of previous experiences to navigate to a goal—directly inspiring replay mechanisms that have been highly successful for the same purpose in machine learning ([Eysenbach et al. 2019](#right-ref-B40), [Hassabis et al. 2017](#right-ref-B68), [Lillicrap et al. 2015](#right-ref-B90), [Rolnick et al. 2019](#right-ref-B143)). Replay is not only a fast-forward mechanism through sequences observed online—the same sequences can also be recapitulated in the reverse order from how they were observed ([Foster & Wilson 2006](#right-ref-B48)) ( [Figure 3a](#f3) , [d](#f3) ), which computational models have found supports planning more effectively than forward replay alone ([Eysenbach et al. 2019](#right-ref-B40), [Mattar & Daw 2018](#right-ref-B102)). On a more abstract level, replay sequences can also be thought of as a neural readout of learning, which are refined over multiple exposures to an environment. [Berners-Lee et al. (2022)](#right-ref-B10) found that replays appear after single exposures and slow down while increasing in resolution with repeated experience. [Widloski & Foster (2022)](#right-ref-B185) examined sequence replay in an environment with barriers and found that replayed sequences consistently respected the constraints of the environment even as the configuration of the barriers changed. Hippocampal sequence generation is also not necessarily contiguous in time; rapid cycling and discontinuous alternation have also been reported online and offline ([Kay et al. 2020](#right-ref-B80), [Wang et al. 2020](#right-ref-B182)).

3.1.  Filtering and Adaptive Selection in Replay

The prevailing hypotheses about replay content hold that replay reinstates either predictive representations or specific past experiences. While replay content reflects planning in some tasks ([Foster 2017](#right-ref-B46), [Ólafsdóttir et al. 2018](#right-ref-B128)), it is divorced from imminent plans in others ([Ambrose et al. 2016](#right-ref-B2), [Gillespie et al. 2021](#right-ref-B55), [Karlsson & Frank 2008](#right-ref-B78), [Singer & Frank 2009](#right-ref-B153)). These nonimmediately useful replay events may nonetheless be useful if replay stores and organizes specific experience for later use ([Gillespie et al. 2021](#right-ref-B55), [Wittkuhn et al. 2021](#right-ref-B187)). The specific-experience model predicts that replay events should resemble sequences that are representative of individual experiences of the environment. However, [Terada et al. (2022)](#right-ref-B165) found that even when prominent random stimuli dominate the animal's specific experiences, representations of stimuli that correspond to unique experiences are not only not reactivated but actively suppressed from replay.

Part of this disconnect may stem from the fact that replay is usually studied in highly regular and deterministic environments, as these are the easiest to realize in the laboratory setting in order to conduct many repeated trials. However, repeated exposures to the same places are not the same in the real world: Natural environments contain both statistical regularities and experience-specific stimuli that do not generalize. Growing evidence suggests that replay in the hippocampus does not simply reproduce previously experienced sequences verbatim but also actively selects and filters those that are important ([Gupta et al. 2010](#right-ref-B66), [McClelland et al. 1995](#right-ref-B103)). Recent papers ([Gillespie et al. 2021](#right-ref-B55), [Grosmark et al. 2021](#right-ref-B64), [Terada et al. 2022](#right-ref-B165)) have found nonrepresentative sampling of experience during both intratask rest and offline periods. [Gillespie et al. (2021)](#right-ref-B55) showed that in a multiarm, goal-oriented learning task, remote experiences were replayed more often than immediately upcoming paths, complicating the replay-as-planning model. Another study of goal-oriented navigation showed that while animals spent the most time physically in the proximity of a reward location in real time, replay reduces this behavioral bias by upsampling regions of space in which less time was spent ([Grosmark et al. 2021](#right-ref-B64)). A study of rodents exploring a track featuring both random and predictable stimuli ( [Figure 4a](#f4) , [d](#f4) ) showed that, despite the salience of random stimuli on every pass through the environment, SWRs recruited primarily the representations of fixed, predictable stimuli ([Terada et al. 2022](#right-ref-B165)) ( [Figure 4b](#f4) , [c](#f4) , [f](#f4) ). Mechanisms such as these may improve the flexibility of the cognitive map by reducing recency bias, that is, overfitting representations to specific features of a proximal task such as fixating only on the imminent goal, neglecting areas of space that are not currently important, or focusing on salient but irrelevant stimuli. Taken together, these observations instead imply a preprogrammed inductive bias of the hippocampus toward forming a cognitive map that is transferable to dissimilar tasks in which different information may need to be retrieved. Multiple experimental ([Ólafsdóttir et al. 2017](#right-ref-B129), [Terada et al. 2022](#right-ref-B165)) and theoretical ([Liao et al. 2022](#right-ref-B89), [Sun et al. 2023](#right-ref-B163)) studies have converged on the conclusion that a gradual transition from specific to generalizable learning occurs with exposure to the environment. We believe this view synthesizes the specific experience and model-based replay views and explains why distal past sequences as well as sequences that have never been observed are replayed.

Figure 4  Hebbian sequence learning, and a possible role for inhibitory plasticity. (a) Schematic of a random cue (RC) task, where mice are presented with sensory cues at random locations and random intertrial intervals (ITIs). (b) Average peri-sharp-wave ripple (peri-SWR) fluorescence (ΔF/F 0) response of axons from cue-responsive CA3 Schaffer collateral (CA3SC) axons during the RC task. Note the gradual suppression of the response magnitude. (c) Average peri-SWR responses for cue-responsive (red) and other (gray) CA3SC axons during stimulus-free periods before and after RC sensory stimulation. Note the selective suppression of cue-responsive axons during SWRs and during stimulation and after. (d) Latent sequences in the world, with noisy observations. (e) Hebbian plasticity embeds sequentially observed pairs into a weight matrix (black +). This also results in the learning of spurious associations (red +). Inhibitory plasticity (bottom) prevents the replay of distractor stimuli. (f) Population activity during online exploration and offline replay. Nongeneralizable stimuli that are represented online are selectively suppressed from consolidation. Panels a–c adapted from [Terada et al. (2022)](#right-ref-B165), and panels d–f adapted from images created with BioRender.com and previously published in [Liao et al. (2022)](#right-ref-B89).

[![
                     Figure 4 
                  ](https://www.annualreviews.org/docserver/ahah/fulltext/neuro/47/1/ne470187.f4_thmb.gif)](https://www.annualreviews.org/docserver/fulltext/neuro/47/1/ne470187.f4.gif)

[Figure 4   
  
Click to view](https://www.annualreviews.org/docserver/fulltext/neuro/47/1/ne470187.f4.gif)

[Download as PowerPoint](https://www.annualreviews.org/docserver/fulltext/neuro/47/1/ne470187.f4.ppt?mimeType=application/vnd.ms-powerpoint)

While the phenomenology of hippocampal sequence learning has been extensively characterized, the precise underlying mechanisms remain unclear. SWRs and the replay sequences they are associated with are traditionally thought to originate in hippocampal area CA3. CA3 is a strongly recurrently connected network, while CA1 has few excitatory-to-excitatory connections, enabling sequences to be learned in CA3 and read out by CA1. [Geiller et al. (2022)](#right-ref-B52) found that when a place field is induced in a single CA1 place cell, an assembly of other CA1 cells simultaneously acquire the same place field, challenging this view and suggesting that intra-areal interactions may play a greater role in CA1 than previously believed. In silico manipulations of a full-scale computational model of CA1 by [Bezaire et al. (2016)](#right-ref-B11) also pointed to a possible disproportionate functional role for the sparse anatomical recurrence: In the model ([Bezaire & Soltesz 2013](#right-ref-B12)), perturbing this sparse recurrence in either direction significantly reduced hippocampal theta power, while ablating it eliminated hippocampal theta completely. The degree to which sequences are preconfigured during development as opposed to learned entirely from scratch remains an open question. It is known that sequences are not immediately present at birth but rather develop gradually and lengthen postnatally after the development of spatially localized firing ([Muessig et al. 2019](#right-ref-B114)). However, in addition to replay following an experience, several recent studies ([Buzsáki & Draguhn 2004](#right-ref-B19), [Dragoi & Tonegawa 2014](#right-ref-B29), [Grosmark & Buzsáki 2016](#right-ref-B63), [McKenzie 2018](#right-ref-B105)) have demonstrated that replay can be detected even before animals have any experience of an environment (c.f. [Silva et al. 2015](#right-ref-B152)). Place cell sequences detected during such preplay events are subsequently found to correspond to trajectories through a novel environment. This preplay may reflect activity in preconfigured cell assemblies primed to participate jointly in future spatial representations. Two recent studies ([Geiller et al. 2022](#right-ref-B52), [Huszar et al. 2022](#right-ref-B70)) demonstrated that developmentally related and functionally interconnected subnetworks of CA1 pyramidal cells have shared in vivo functional properties: They form adjacent place fields during navigation and are more likely to co-fire during SWRs. These results suggest that preexisting and developmentally established circuit connectivity constraints impose a developmental prior on learning but do not determine it completely.

3.2.  Plasticity Rules for Many-Shot Sequence Learning

A drawback of the BTSP rule is that order within the behavioral-timescale plasticity window cannot be discriminated, as inputs to a cell spanning several seconds are potentiated. A further problem is that biological sequences do not have a fixed length, so sequential relationships that span timescales longer than a plasticity window set by biophysical time constants would not be learnable. Therefore, while a single-shot, fixed time-window learning rule such as BTSP is well suited for learning discrete concepts, it is poorly suited to sequence learning.

Hebbian rules such as STDP ( [Figures 3c](#f3) and [4e](#f4) ), on the other hand, are well suited to this type of learning. The fast timescale of STDP requires many exposures for learning; each pairing is associated with a small weight change. This is advantageous because many observed associations are spurious; repeated experience helps to discriminate spurious from consistent associations. STDP is a natural plasticity rule for learning sequential relationships: In a recurrent network, a synapse from a place cell to its behavioral successor will be potentiated. A limitation of this model of sequence learning is that the slow pace of real behavior on which an associated pair co-occurs may not match the milliseconds-long biophysically determined Hebbian plasticity window. Two mechanisms may help bridge these temporal gaps. First, a subset of place cells termed time cells have been found to respond to stimuli with a certain time delay, which could bring together neural representations of stimuli that are not contiguous in time ([Eichenbaum 2014](#right-ref-B35)). Second, embedding sequences within theta cycles ( [Figure 3b](#f3) ) suborganizes individual spikes in a population of neurons to cycles of a shared clock on a millisecond timescale that enables the learning of ordered sequences via STDP ([Losonczy et al. 2010](#right-ref-B96)).

[Mishra et al. (2016)](#right-ref-B111) showed a symmetric STDP rule in CA3, in contrast to the classically reported asymmetric rule ([Markram et al. 1997](#right-ref-B101)). Computational modeling studies ([Ecker et al. 2022](#right-ref-B32), [Liao et al. 2022](#right-ref-B89), [Milstein et al. 2023](#right-ref-B110)) have subsequently shown a potential role for this rule in enabling the spontaneous generation of both forward and backward replay, originally reported by [Foster & Wilson (2006)](#right-ref-B48); though of note, different replay directions serve different functions in several computational models ([Mattar & Daw 2018](#right-ref-B102)).

4.  PLASTICITY AT OTHER TIMES AND PLACES

We have thus far discussed two distinct mechanisms of hippocampal plasticity, which have predominantly been studied at excitatory terminals during online learning. We now broaden our scope to also consider plasticity at inhibitory synapses and during offline rest. The study of Hebbian learning rules in the hippocampus and elsewhere in the brain has mainly centered around excitatory-to-excitatory connections ([Hebb 1949](#right-ref-B69), [Kullmann & Lamsa 2007](#right-ref-B85)), most famously the classic finding of [Markram et al. (1997)](#right-ref-B101) that causal pre-/postsynaptic spike pairs lead to synaptic potentiation, while anticausal pairs lead to synaptic depression, in proportion to the time between the spikes. A smaller but growing body of literature has discussed the possibility of plasticity in the inhibitory population as a contributor to learning.

Different interneuron subtypes are known to have different roles in the control of place cell dynamics ([McKenzie 2018](#right-ref-B105), [Royer et al. 2012](#right-ref-B147), [Tzilivaki et al. 2023](#right-ref-B171)). [Turi et al. (2019)](#right-ref-B169) showed that vasoactive intestinal peptide–expressing interneurons reorganize their dynamics with learning. Large-scale studies of many simultaneously recorded hippocampal interneuron subtypes have shown that certain interneurons exhibited spatially tuned activity ([Dudok et al. 2021a](#right-ref-B30),[b](#right-ref-B31); [Geiller et al. 2020](#right-ref-B53); [Vancura et al. 2023](#right-ref-B176)).These findings demonstrate that the modulation of interneuron activity can have profound and nuanced effects on a circuit. It is natural to ask, is this modulation used for learning in the brain, and if so, by what mechanism? Learning requires targeted changes in response to a certain stimulus, the so-called credit assignment problem. The plasticity of glutamatergic synapses onto inhibitory neurons ([McMahon & Kauer 1997](#right-ref-B106)) (excitatory-to-inhibitory plasticity) is a natural extension of excitatory-to-excitatory plasticity but would not on its own be sufficient to implement learning in an inhibitory population: Hippocampal interneuron populations have been reported to be highly synchronous ([Geiller et al. 2020](#right-ref-B53), [Vancura et al. 2023](#right-ref-B176)), so such input modifications would result in output changes diluted across the entire pyramidal cell network. However, another mechanism by which this could occur is weight modulation directly at GABAergic, rather than glutamatergic, synapses. In vitro experiments in hippocampal slices have reported long-term plasticity of GABAergic synapses ([Gaiarsa et al. 2002](#right-ref-B51)). Different authors have reported different rules regarding the precise nature of this inhibitory plasticity, whether potentiating ([Caillard et al. 1999a](#right-ref-B21), [Nusser et al. 1998](#right-ref-B124)), depressing ([Caillard et al. 1999b](#right-ref-B22); [Chevaleyre & Castillo 2003](#right-ref-B24); [Stelzer et al. 1987](#right-ref-B160), [1994](#right-ref-B159)), or rate dependent ([Gubellini et al. 2001](#right-ref-B65)). Of particular interest, [D'amour & Froemke (2015)](#right-ref-B26) showed a symmetric spike time–dependent long-term plasticity rule for the potentiation of inhibitory synapses in auditory cortex, paralleling the symmetric CA3 plasticity kernel ([Mishra et al. 2016](#right-ref-B111)).

Interestingly, a large body of modeling literature has predicted a role for inhibitory plasticity in learning in the hippocampus ([Liao et al. 2022](#right-ref-B89); [Vogels et al. 2011](#right-ref-B181), [2013](#right-ref-B180)), though models also exist that reproduce hippocampal phenomena absent any inhibitory plasticity ([Ecker et al. 2022](#right-ref-B32), [Nicola & Clopath 2017](#right-ref-B117), [2019](#right-ref-B118)), suggesting a more subtle role for this type of plasticity. Modeling work by [Liao et al. (2022)](#right-ref-B89) proposed the selective offline reactivation of pertinent information in the presence of noise reported by [Terada et al. (2022)](#right-ref-B165), which is not possible without inhibitory plasticity. The requirement that the brain's memory system be both expressive and flexible may provide insight into why a plasticity mechanism, in particular one of inhibitory synapses, supports robust learning in the face of distractions—the brain cannot know a priori which stimuli are important until it has sufficiently sampled the environment. The same stimulus that is a distraction in one context may be essential to understanding the structure of the world in the next. Therefore, the hippocampus must be able to robustly represent all stimuli during online learning as they are received, then selectively suppress those that are not generalizable in order to consolidate representations that are likely to be useful in the future.

Memory consolidation refers to the transfer of memories from short-term storage in the hippocampus to long-term storage, primarily in cortex, and requires reactivation of the representations to be consolidated. Most investigations of plasticity have focused on changes during online learning, that is, while an animal is performing a task, while memory consolidation primarily occurs in the offline period. SWRs have emerged as a biomarker of memory consolidation ([Buzsáki 2015](#right-ref-B18)) and have been recorded in multiple brain areas beyond the hippocampus, including cortical areas, in cross-species studies ([Buzsáki 2015](#right-ref-B18), [Buzsáki et al. 2013](#right-ref-B20), A.A. [Liu et al. 2022](#right-ref-B92), [Norman et al. 2019](#right-ref-B122), [Skaggs et al. 2007](#right-ref-B154), [Vaz et al. 2019](#right-ref-B177)). The hippocampus and prefrontal cortex exhibit complementary representations during tasks, with improving performance across tasks suggestive of knowledge transfer ([Samborska et al. 2022](#right-ref-B149)). It is known that replay is also coordinated between the hippocampus and cortical areas ([Euston et al. 2007](#right-ref-B39), [Ji & Wilson 2007](#right-ref-B75), [Peyrache et al. 2009](#right-ref-B134), [Rothschild et al. 2017](#right-ref-B146)), which has been proposed to underlie compositional inference ([Schwartenbeck et al. 2023](#right-ref-B150)). [Sugden et al. (2020)](#right-ref-B161) found reactivations of cue-associated patterns coupled to SWRs in cortex, with intriguing selectivity for task-relevant neurons and suppression of task-irrelevant neurons, paralleling the findings of [Terada et al. (2022)](#right-ref-B165). Not all memories are consolidated; computational modeling studies have argued that selective consolidation is necessary because consolidating everything would lead to overfitting ([Sun et al. 2023](#right-ref-B163)). This selective culling could therefore represent the mechanism by which useful stimuli are preferentially chosen for consolidation ([Liao et al. 2022](#right-ref-B89), [Sun et al. 2023](#right-ref-B163), [Terada et al. 2022](#right-ref-B165)).

It is also known that significant network reorganization occurs during rest. [Sadowski et al. (2016)](#right-ref-B148) showed that pattern reactivation in SWRs during sleep induces plasticity, while [Ego-Stengel & Wilson (2010)](#right-ref-B33) and others have shown that disrupting this activity impairs learning. Consolidation requires plasticity of the interareal projections between hippocampus and cortex, but whether synaptic weight changes also occur during rest periods within the hippocampus (offline plasticity) has not been studied. SWRs have been shown to enable synaptic potentiation in vivo, as depolarization during SWRs can increase responsivity during subsequent SWRs ([King et al. 1999](#right-ref-B83)). [Jadhav et al. (2012)](#right-ref-B73) found that if awake SWRs are disrupted, learning is impaired even though online spatial representations and postexperience replay remain intact. In vitro experiments indicate that long-term synaptic potentiation is observed when specific patterns of spiking that occur during SWRs are paired with these network oscillatory events ([Sadowski et al. 2016](#right-ref-B148)). Similarly, long-term plasticity of intrinsic dendritic excitability was reported in vitro following SWR-like (i.e., spatially clustered and temporally synchronous) activation of excitatory synapses ([Losonczy et al. 2008](#right-ref-B95)). Under this framework, SWRs may promote long-term potentiation in vivo to strengthen CA1 pyramidal cell assemblies that are coactive within a SWR, while homeostatically depressing synapses unrelated to ensemble reactivation ([Fauth & Van Rossum 2019](#right-ref-B42), [Norimoto et al. 2018](#right-ref-B121)). Thus, rapid place cell sequences observed during replay might be optimal for inducing plasticity in postsynaptic targets ([Buzsáki 2015](#right-ref-B18)), but direct in vivo evidence for this is currently lacking. This is a critical knowledge gap as animals spend a significant amount of time immobile, and reactivated representations change over time. We speculate that the hippocampus refines its replayed representations, amplifying the signal and attenuating noise, before transferring them to cortex. [Nguyen et al. (2024)](#right-ref-B116) found that cortical reactivations more closely resembled future responses, while [King et al. (1999)](#right-ref-B83) found that depolarization during one SWR increased responsivity during subsequent SWRs, strongly suggesting that plasticity has occurred in the period between the most recent stimulus presentation and the offline reactivation. Taken together, these data suggest the hypothesis that silently occurring plasticity in the offline period may represent a major and underexplored facet of memory consolidation.

Intriguingly, [Noguchi et al. (2022)](#right-ref-B120) showed that inhibitory activity preceding and during SWRs determines the sequence of pyramidal cell firing. Whether the inhibitory control itself evolves over the course of learning remains a fascinating open question—if so, it would provide evidence that these diverse plasticity mechanisms interact in ways that we are still far from understanding.

5.  CONCLUSIONS

As humans and other animals explore the world, we acquire a mental simulacrum of our surroundings unconsciously, even involuntarily—and even in the absence of an explicit directive to do so ([Tolman 1932](#right-ref-B166)). The cognitive map metaphor introduced by [Tolman (1948)](#right-ref-B167) to explain this phenomenon has been highly influential in the interpretation of the hippocampal code, although the precise nature of this map and how it is learned remain subject to many interpretations ([Behrens et al. 2018](#right-ref-B7); [Stachenfeld et al. 2017](#right-ref-B157); [Whittington et al. 2020](#right-ref-B184), [2022](#right-ref-B183)). On the other hand, the deficits of many neurological and psychiatric conditions, including temporal lobe epilepsy, schizophrenia, and autism, have been linked to impairments in hippocampal coding ([Altimus et al. 2015](#right-ref-B1), [Engel 1996](#right-ref-B38), [Hadjiabadi et al. 2021](#right-ref-B67), [Lenck-Santini & Scott 2015](#right-ref-B87), [Liotta et al. 2011](#right-ref-B91), [Marafiga et al. 2020](#right-ref-B100), [Nour et al. 2021](#right-ref-B123), [Paz & Huguenard 2015](#right-ref-B132), [Reid & Stewart 1997](#right-ref-B142), [Sparks et al. 2020](#right-ref-B156), [Suh et al. 2013](#right-ref-B162), [Zaremba et al. 2017](#right-ref-B190)).

BTSP and Hebbian plasticity, despite falling under the common umbrella of learning, are highly distinct in their mechanics, timescale, and underlying philosophy. BTSP can be thought of as the supervised statistical learning of a binary classifier by a single cell: A hippocampal pyramidal cell receives an instructive signal and then learns a new concept in a single shot by aligning weights on its rich dendritic basis set to a recently experienced ensemble of stimuli, unifying them into a new receptive field ([Bittner et al. 2017](#right-ref-B15), [2015](#right-ref-B14); [Milstein et al. 2021](#right-ref-B109)). Viewed in this way, it is perhaps not surprising that place field–like representations have been reported for many nonspatial concepts ([Baraduc et al. 2019](#right-ref-B6), [Benna & Fusi 2021](#right-ref-B9), [O'Keefe & Dostrovsky 1971](#right-ref-B126), [O'Keefe & Nadel 1978](#right-ref-B127), [Quiroga et al. 2005](#right-ref-B140)). Likewise, it strikes us as unlikely that the hippocampus would be uniquely endowed with this rule. Single-shot learning is not unique to the hippocampus; the rapid emergence of feature selectivity has been described in many other brain regions, particularly sensory areas, and the minimal architecture needed to support a BTSP-like rule is found throughout the brain. We suggest that this type of rule could be a fruitful lens through which to examine the emergence of feature selectivity in many brain areas.

The hippocampus also learns sequences, initially anchored in recent specific experiences, modified and generalized over multiple exposures, and recapitulated offline in the form of replay ([Berners-Lee et al. 2022](#right-ref-B10), [Liao et al. 2022](#right-ref-B89), [Ólafsdóttir et al. 2017](#right-ref-B129), [Sun et al. 2023](#right-ref-B163), [Terada et al. 2022](#right-ref-B165)); we believe this phenomenon reflects an inductive bias toward replaying and consolidating generalizable representations, although the mechanisms that enable the transition from specific to general remain to be elucidated. In the same vein, reactivation of recent experience has been observed in prefrontal, motor, and primary sensory cortices ([Ji & Wilson 2007](#right-ref-B75), [Nguyen et al. 2024](#right-ref-B116), [Peyrache et al. 2009](#right-ref-B134), [Rothschild et al. 2017](#right-ref-B146), [Sugden et al. 2020](#right-ref-B161)), often coordinated with hippocampal replay. Ripples have also been described in many of the same areas ([Grenier et al. 2001](#right-ref-B59), [Khodagholy et al. 2017](#right-ref-B81)). Whether sequential reactivation and cortical fast oscillations also coincide extrahippocampally represents an unexplored avenue for future research.

While we have discussed single-shot learning mainly through the lens of BTSP and incremental learning mainly through the lens of Hebbian plasticity and sequence learning, it is important to note that this is not an absolute distinction. Place fields are gradually refined and stabilized over time by Hebbian plasticity ([Ekstrom et al. 2001](#right-ref-B36), [Mehta et al. 1997](#right-ref-B107)), although recent work has suggested that BTSP may play a role in this refinement process as well ([Madar et al. 2023](#right-ref-B97), [Vaidya et al. 2023](#right-ref-B174)). The factors controlling place field stability over time represent an important area of study ([Grosmark et al. 2021](#right-ref-B64))— [Vaidya et al. (2023)](#right-ref-B174) found stable and transient place cells on each day of recording and suggested that a higher rate of BTSP stabilized the code by stabilizing informative pyramidal cells. Sequence replay can also be observed immediately after a single experience, with content anchored to the specifics of that experience ([Berners-Lee et al. 2022](#right-ref-B10)). We propose that a speed-amplitude trade-off may underpin both STDP and BTSP, whereby the magnitude of weight change in a plasticity event is proportional to the expected information gained: BTSP integrates information over a long timescale and hence is associated with large-amplitude weight changes, enabling learning in a single shot; STDP integrates relatively little information contained in single spikes, and hence is associated with small-amplitude weight changes, requiring many exposures to learn.

The learning of single concepts and sequences, on its surface, may appear to cover only a small fraction of our rich subjective experience of memory, even for only a single brain area such as the hippocampus. In combination and sufficiently iterated, however, these two mechanisms impart enough flexibility to learn worlds of high complexity. The ability to draw samples from a learned distribution is known to be computationally extremely powerful, as it enables a wide range of queries to be executed on the underlying model, without explicitly storing it ([Gelfand & Smith 1990](#right-ref-B54)). A system that can encode concepts and the pairwise sequential relationships between them is in principle expressive enough to represent any graph. Graphs are a highly flexible and natural general model of complex worlds, capturing distinct concepts as well as the topological relationships between them, and therefore feature prominently in models of cognitive maps ([Behrens et al. 2018](#right-ref-B7); [Stachenfeld et al. 2017](#right-ref-B157); [Whittington et al. 2020](#right-ref-B184), [2022](#right-ref-B183)). We also note that, unlike graph or metric learning in the typical machine learning setting, the concepts themselves are also learned from a continuous input stream.

Of course, learning is more complicated than what we have been able to review here. There remain broad subfields within learning that we have not discussed—for instance, the role of neuromodulation in learning and plasticity ([Kaufman et al. 2020](#right-ref-B79)), or how the 3D subcellular architecture of cells shapes learning ([Gonzalez et al. 2022](#right-ref-B57), [2023](#right-ref-B58)). While technologies for recording the suprathreshold activity of single cells and populations are relatively mature, advances in techniques for interrogating subthreshold activity, neurotransmitter release, and other forms of biochemical signaling in vivo, particularly in subcellular compartments, will undoubtedly lead to major biological breakthroughs in the next few years. Genetically encoded voltage indicators have proliferated in recent years ([Z. Liu et al. 2022](#right-ref-B93), [Villette et al. 2019](#right-ref-B178)), and genetically encoded calcium indicators have become faster and more sensitive ([Zhang et al. 2023](#right-ref-B191)). Direct sensors of neurotransmitters such as dopamine and glutamate have also become available ([Wu et al. 2022](#right-ref-B188)). However, advancements in rapid scanning and random access are still needed to take full advantage of the temporal and dynamic resolution these sensors offer while maintaining high throughput. We are seeing a second revolution of in vivo two-photon and single-cell targeted optogenetics, which will usher in an era of all-optical physiology in which synaptic plasticity rules may be directly interrogated in vivo ([Gonzalez et al. 2023](#right-ref-B58)). At the same time, behavioral experiments have become much richer with the popularization of virtual reality ([Bowler & Losonczy 2023](#right-ref-B16), [Nieh et al. 2021](#right-ref-B119), [Priestley et al. 2022](#right-ref-B139)). The majority of the evidence for cognitive mapping thus far is closely tied to physical mapping. These advances should spur investigators to add richness and multisensory realism to our experimental tasks. In order to begin to narrow the gap from simple spatial tasks to humans’ astonishing capacity for learning complex concepts and the relationships between them, future experiments must begin to examine the learning of knowledge spaces beyond those that can be realized in physical space.

SUMMARY POINTS

1. 1\. Behavioral timescale synaptic plasticity (BTSP) allows the acquisition of new concepts from single experiences.
2. 2\. Hebbian plasticity supports the incremental assimilation of sequential information over many trials.
3. 3\. A trade-off between speed and weight change may underpin both BTSP and spike timing–dependent plasticity.
4. 4\. Replay sequences exhibit distinct statistics and content compared to sequences observed online.
5. 5\. Progress in the spatiotemporal resolution and throughput of methods for in vivo recording and targeted manipulation has allowed us to dissect learning dynamics in increasingly finer detail.

FUTURE ISSUES

1. 1\. Whether plasticity continues during replay, and what principles govern its dynamics, remains to be elucidated.
2. 2\. Whether a three-factor rule similar to behavioral timescale synaptic plasticity may also play a role in other brain areas remains unknown.
3. 3\. Experimental and modeling work is needed to clarify the expressivity and limits of learning with hippocampal rules.
4. 4\. Inhibitory and offline plasticity may play an important role in learning in the hippocampus, though further investigation is needed in both areas.
5. 5\. In vivo imaging and manipulation tools currently in use have not yet reached their full potential. Technological advances will likely yield high returns in the areas of fast scanning and random access imaging.
